  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_5b9eba70-6ef5-45ed-82ca-38f24a46c352'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);

<span id="freeTextContainer6898946877125936180"><div>When a brilliant, driven industrialist harnesses the cutting edge of quantum physics to enable people everywhere, at trivial cost, to see one another at all times: around every corner, through every wall, into everyone's most private, hidden, and even intimate moments. It amounts to the sudden and complete abolition of human privacy--forever. <br/><br/>Then, as society reels, the sa</div></span>
  <span id="freeText6898946877125936180" style="display:none"><div>When a brilliant, driven industrialist harnesses the cutting edge of quantum physics to enable people everywhere, at trivial cost, to see one another at all times: around every corner, through every wall, into everyone's most private, hidden, and even intimate moments. It amounts to the sudden and complete abolition of human privacy--forever. <br /><br />Then, as society reels, the same technology proves able to look backwards in time as well. What happens next is a story only Arthur C. Clarke and Stephen Baxter could tell. <i>The Light of Other Days</i> is a novel that will change your view of what it is to be human.<br /></div></span>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1966531152?book_show_action=true">Apr 09, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Apatt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Apatt" href="/user/show/2457095-apatt">Apatt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2457095-apatt?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1966531152" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12559633331184647508"><i>“I grew up with enough bad pop-science shows. A wormhole is a shortcut through a fourth dimension. You have to cut a chunk out of our three-dimensional space and join it onto another such chunk.”</i><br/><br/>You don’t normally get this kind of “pop-science” from Clarke or Baxter. It makes a nice change. This bit of expository dialogue is also of “pop-science” level:<br/><br/><i>“A wormhole mouth is a sphere, floating freely in space. A three-dimensional excision. If we succeed with the expansion, for the first time we’ll</i></span>
  <span id="freeText12559633331184647508" style="display:none"><i>“I grew up with enough bad pop-science shows. A wormhole is a shortcut through a fourth dimension. You have to cut a chunk out of our three-dimensional space and join it onto another such chunk.”</i><br><br>You don’t normally get this kind of “pop-science” from Clarke or Baxter. It makes a nice change. This bit of expository dialogue is also of “pop-science” level:<br><br><i>“A wormhole mouth is a sphere, floating freely in space. A three-dimensional excision. If we succeed with the expansion, for the first time we’ll be able to see our wormhole mouth—with a hand lens”</i><br><br><i>The Light of Other Days</i> is hard sci-fi done right, very accessible, yet interesting, intelligent and mind blowing. The basic conceit is the invention the “WormCam”, a camera that can see through wormhole mouths which can be placed anywhere in the world, or even out of it, all it needs is the geographical coordinates of what you want to see. Initially, the WormCam is only used by the corporation that funds its research, then government agencies get wind of it and it is used to track criminals with great success. Soon details of this invention are leaked to the general public and this eventually leads to mass production and public dissemination. The WormCam becomes a world-changing product of internet proportions. Once everybody has one, the idea of privacy becomes obsolete. The novel charts the impact on culture and social mores in fascinating details; for example, public nudity becomes commonplace as everybody can be seen at any time. Many politicians resign as secrecy also becomes practically obsolete as a concept.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1491750222i/22442648._SX540_.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br>This idea of constant, worldwide surveillance without limits and the socio-political ramifications are epic. However, it soon transpires that the surveillance aspect of the WormCam is just scratching the surface of what the technology is capable of. The inventor of the WormCam soon finds a way to connect wormholes into the past! Past misdeeds are no longer safe from scrutiny but, more interestingly, users can view any event in the past, going back as far as they like. People use it to look up their remote ancestors then they look into the lives of historical figures including Abraham Lincoln and Jesus Christ.<br><br><i>The Light of Other Days</i> took me by surprise, it starts off as a story about super surveillance then morphs into an <i>almost</i> time traveling story. Fortunately, the scientist cannot find a way to affect the past in any way, only passive observation is possible. This saves the book from becoming just another time traveling tale. In addition to the main plotline about the WormCam the novel is also set in the near future when most species of animals are extinct, food is becoming scarce and nations go to war over water supplies. Worse still a giant asteroid is heading for Earth and is projected to arrive in 500 years, the impact is expected to be a mass extinction event. The main characters are also going through personal crises of their own but I won’t go into that.<br><br>There are so many ideas in this book, so many that some of them fall by the wayside, not sufficiently explored; the development of hive minds, virtual reality, cloning, uploading personalities etc. Even the asteroid’s threat to all life on Earth is a mere subplot. Besides the technological speculations, Clarke &amp; Baxter also raise several philosophical and moral issues, the pros and cons of surveillance, transparency, social taboos, embellishment of historical figures etc. These two authors are not known for nuanced characterization, but they (probably mainly Baxter) did a pretty good job with Kate, the journalist who is an interesting and multifaceted individual during the first half of the book but loses much of the agency in the second half. Hiram the corporation CEO is larger than life but is a one-note character. <br><br><i>The Light of Other Days</i> is an excellent read, the science, the philosophy, social impact etc. provide plenty of food for thought. My only complaint, as mentioned earlier, is that some of the ideas are left unexplored; this is only a minor issue, however, better too many ideas than too few. Arthur C. Clarke and Stephen Baxter work together very well, and I hope to read more of their collaborations.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1491750621i/22442692.jpg" width="100" height="100" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1491750621i/22442692.jpg" width="100" height="100" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1491750621i/22442692.jpg" width="100" height="100" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1491750621i/22442692.jpg" width="100" height="100" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Notes:</b><br>• Considering the authors are atheists the depiction of the historical Jesus is surprisingly sympathetic and positive. The WormCam study of his life finds that he was merely human but a great man, and an inspirational figure; probably the most inspirational figure ever. This portrayal is based on <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1531514.Jesus_A_Life" title="Jesus A Life by A.N. Wilson" rel="nofollow noopener"> Jesus</a> by A. N. Wilson.<br><br>• The book is inspired by a short story by underrated sci-fi author, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/130541.Bob_Shaw" title="Bob Shaw" rel="nofollow noopener"> Bob Shaw</a>, and is even dedicated to him. This short story was expanded to by Shaw in to the excellent novel <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/939190.Other_Days__Other_Eyes" title="Other Days, Other Eyes by Bob Shaw" rel="nofollow noopener"> Other Days, Other Eyes</a>.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1491750621i/22442692.jpg" width="100" height="100" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1491750621i/22442692.jpg" width="100" height="100" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1491750621i/22442692.jpg" width="100" height="100" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1491750621i/22442692.jpg" width="100" height="100" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Quotes:</b><br><br>‘Empty’ space is actually full, full of fluctuating energy fields. And these fields manifest themselves as particles: photons, electron-positron pairs, quarks…They flash into a brief existence, bankrolled by borrowed mass-energy, then disappear as the law of conservation of energy reasserts itself. We humans see space and energy and matter from far above, like an astronaut flying over an ocean. We are too high to see the waves, the flecks of foam they carry. But they are there.<br><br>“They call it Wheeler instability. Wormholes aren’t naturally stable. A wormhole mouth’s gravity pulls in photons, accelerates them to high energy, and that energized radiation bombards the throat and causes it to pinch off. It’s the effect you have to counter with Casimir-effect negative energy, to keep open even the smallest wormholes.”<br><br>Instant-access WormCam technology or not, it was going to take a long time before the news-watching public was weaned off the interpretative presence of a reporter interposing herself before some breaking news story.<br><br>But Britain was declining. As part of unified Europe—deprived of tools of macroeconomic policy like control of exchange and interest rates, and yet unsheltered by the imperfectly integrated greater economy—the British government was unable to arrest a sharp economic collapse. At last, in 2010, social unrest and climate collapse forced Britain out of the European Union, and the United Kingdom fell apart, Scotland going its own separate way.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12559633331184647508" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_181564733" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/181564733" />
    <a title="Scot McAtee" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5353456-scot-mcatee"><img alt="Scot McAtee" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1374715786p2/5353456.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/181564733?book_show_action=true">Jul 03, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Scot McAtee" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Scot McAtee" href="/user/show/5353456-scot-mcatee">Scot McAtee</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer181564733" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14336830725608970495">This is what sci-fi is all about. Highly recommend it.<br/><br/>When the world discovers how and when it will end, the decline of humanity begins immediately. Most people become nothing more than animals seeking hedonistic pleasures, as if they believed they were going to die that day. But one fellow keeps his wits about him and continues to invent. His greatest invention, the worm cam, alters the trajectory of humanity as much as the impending natural disaster.<br/><br/>One can't help but link the worm cam and it'</span>
  <span id="freeText14336830725608970495" style="display:none">This is what sci-fi is all about. Highly recommend it.<br /><br />When the world discovers how and when it will end, the decline of humanity begins immediately. Most people become nothing more than animals seeking hedonistic pleasures, as if they believed they were going to die that day. But one fellow keeps his wits about him and continues to invent. His greatest invention, the worm cam, alters the trajectory of humanity as much as the impending natural disaster.<br /><br />One can't help but link the worm cam and it's impact to current events. I'll leave it to the reader to make the connection, but it's so obvious once you start this story, that the reader can't help but think about the state of our world today.<br /><br />I loved the Jesus scene and the explanation of why the day darkened-- it's classic. Also, the search for answers that leads our hero to the extreme past (while most people become voyeurs) gives us the hope that even though history has and will repeat itself, life goes on, even if it's not how we might expect.<br /><br />Is that a bad thing? I still wonder that.<br /><br />This book engaged me as much as any sci-fi book ever has. It is so timely and relevant that I am reminded of it constantly-- at work, at home, when watching tv or reading or (of course) surfing the internet.<br /><br />This book is a must read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14336830725608970495" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer3791906990420553390">An interesting concept that quickly descends into dreck. I expected better from a couple of genre masters. The writing style wanders, a common thing when more than one author is involved. Rather than a coherent science fiction story, this book lurches between sections of story, science, and case studies that with work, could have been turned into a novel.<br/><br/>The authors use wormholes as a device that enables universal surveillance, including reaching into the past. This could have been terribly inte</span>
  <span id="freeText3791906990420553390" style="display:none">An interesting concept that quickly descends into dreck. I expected better from a couple of genre masters. The writing style wanders, a common thing when more than one author is involved. Rather than a coherent science fiction story, this book lurches between sections of story, science, and case studies that with work, could have been turned into a novel.<br /><br />The authors use wormholes as a device that enables universal surveillance, including reaching into the past. This could have been terribly interesting; instead, the choices the authors made varied between boring, pandering, and predictable. Religion is trashed, but in the end, all the authors can think up is to have the great god science engineer resurrection for everyone.</span>

<div id="review_655093681" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/655093681" />
    <a title="erforscherin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20128263-erforscherin"><img alt="erforscherin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1422575960p2/20128263.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/655093681?book_show_action=true">Jun 30, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="erforscherin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="erforscherin" href="/user/show/20128263-erforscherin">erforscherin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20128263-erforscherin?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20128263-erforscherin?shelf=speculative-fiction">speculative-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20128263-erforscherin?shelf=reread">reread</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20128263-erforscherin?shelf=read-before-2013">read-before-2013</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20128263-erforscherin?shelf=old-favorites">old-favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20128263-erforscherin?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20128263-erforscherin?shelf=author-male">author-male</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20128263-erforscherin?shelf=format-ebook">format-ebook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20128263-erforscherin?shelf=highly-recommended">highly-recommended</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20128263-erforscherin?shelf=rating-10-stars">rating-10-stars</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer655093681" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1086563906905972930">I first read this book in summer 2008, and probably not a month has gone by since then that this book hasn't popped into my mind, for one reason or another. The technology and social issues discussed here (particularly regarding the ever-evolving definition of privacy in a society where technology allows everyone to observe everyone at all times) were was a good 25+ years ahead of their time, and are still enormously relevant today.<br/><br/>Yes, the characters are pretty flat, but as with most science-fi</span>
  <span id="freeText1086563906905972930" style="display:none">I first read this book in summer 2008, and probably not a month has gone by since then that this book hasn't popped into my mind, for one reason or another. The technology and social issues discussed here (particularly regarding the ever-evolving definition of privacy in a society where technology allows everyone to observe everyone at all times) were was a good 25+ years ahead of their time, and are still enormously relevant today.<br /><br />Yes, the characters are pretty flat, but as with most science-fiction books of its time, the emphasis is really on the ideas -- and while there are many books since which have discussed privacy in the context of cybersecurity/technological advancement, I've never seen another examine this from a long-term, whole-society point of view... and to me, that alone is worth the price of admission.<br /><br />I can't say enough good things about this book. I'm not sure that I would qualify it as life-changing in my case, but very few others have had more impact.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1086563906905972930" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_107505038" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/107505038" />
    <a title="Aimee" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3532247-aimee"><img alt="Aimee" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1270236883p2/3532247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/107505038?book_show_action=true">Jun 17, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aimee" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aimee" href="/user/show/3532247-aimee">Aimee</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer107505038" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10114547197799718926">Well, it was an interesting idea for a book: quantum physics allow instantaneous transmissions of data across space - cool enough. Then, because of distance-time equivalence in a quantum universe, scientists are able to start beaming transmissions from anywhere in time as well as space. The technology turns almost everyone in the world into a paparazzo of everyone else, and many people also retreat into historical voyeurism. A few people cope with the total loss of privacy by seeking newer, bett</span>
  <span id="freeText10114547197799718926" style="display:none">Well, it was an interesting idea for a book: quantum physics allow instantaneous transmissions of data across space - cool enough. Then, because of distance-time equivalence in a quantum universe, scientists are able to start beaming transmissions from anywhere in time as well as space. The technology turns almost everyone in the world into a paparazzo of everyone else, and many people also retreat into historical voyeurism. A few people cope with the total loss of privacy by seeking newer, better ways to hide from the eyes of the rest of the world, while others use the new technology to link minds to create the Joined, a hypercollective of men and women who submerge their personalities into a greater pool of knowledge and experiences.<br /><br />Sounds cool, right? Well, sort of. I certainly think it <i>could</i> have been cool. I wanted to read more about the collapse of world religions, the battle against the profound loss of civil liberties, the beginnings and development of the Joined. Instead (except for an in-depth sojourn into the life of Jesus) these issues are merely glanced at in favor of the honestly pretty dull personal lives of the main characters: Kate Manzoni, the tough-as-nails Girl Reporter with a heart, and Bobby Patterson, the emotionally stunted and child-like son of the business genius whose company developed all this tech in the first place. Bobby has a couple of dark secret that even he doesn't know about himself (both of which I figured out within approximately eight seconds). Kate and Bobby could have gone and died in a fire for as much as I care about their part in the story. I would have vastly preferred the events to revolve more around Mary, Bobby's half-sister (sort of) and his half-brother (sort of) David, both of whom I felt were under-utilized except as plot devices. The ending also felt super-tacked-on, but that could have been avoided with more development of the Joined during the latter half of the novel.<br /><br />I was also surprised by how amateurish the writing sounded sometimes, because, hello, Arthur C. Clarke? But maybe I'll blame that on Stephen Baxter instead.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10114547197799718926" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_109776247" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/109776247" />
    <a title="John" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1478144-john"><img alt="John" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442450473p2/1478144.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/109776247?book_show_action=true">Jul 02, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John" href="/user/show/1478144-john">John</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780312871994" href="/book/show/939179.The_Light_of_Other_Days">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer109776247&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer109776247" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9773692668934592903">This was a curious experience. The text reads like an Arthur C. Clarke novel (with all the failings and virtues this implies) as written by Steve Baxter (with all the failings and virtues that <i>this</i> implies). Since that's presumably exactly what it is, I guess I shouldn't have been so surprised by the effect, but somehow I'd expected something more of a stylistic amalgamation.<br/> <p>
  The underlying premise is that wormholes can be stabilized sufficiently that enough information can be transmitted thr</p></span>
  <span id="freeText9773692668934592903" style="display:none">This was a curious experience. The text reads like an Arthur C. Clarke novel (with all the failings and virtues this implies) as written by Steve Baxter (with all the failings and virtues that <i>this</i> implies). Since that's presumably exactly what it is, I guess I shouldn't have been so surprised by the effect, but somehow I'd expected something more of a stylistic amalgamation.<br /> <p>
  The underlying premise is that wormholes can be stabilized sufficiently that enough information can be transmitted through them to convey pictures of distant events. Society is revolutionized as, thanks to invisible, omnipresent Wormcams, privacy becomes a thing of the past -- and even more so when the next logical step is taken: the opening up of the entirety of earth's past to the Wormcam, which enables a sort of VR time travel. History is rewritten, crime plummets as clearup rates approach 100%, politicians resign or suicide in droves, millions become hi-tech peeping toms . . . There is a sort of soap-opera plot involving the communications entrepreneur behind these technological breakthroughs, his sons and other family members. All this is played out against a backdrop of humankind's fatalistic knowledge that in just a few hundred years a cometary object called the Wormwood (confusingly, bearing in mind the novel's about Wormcams) will smite our planet, sterilizing it to a depth of many miles. As you might expect given the authorship, there's a long visionary chapter at the end during which our evolutionary ancestry is traced back by Wormcam "travelers" all the way back to the first algal cell -- and even beyond.<br /> 
</p><p>
  But this indicates what for me is a problem with the book. Yes, I can buy it that for a lot of people the big initial appeal of the Wormcam might be that you could watch the neighbours screwing, just as the novel indicates; but one of the uses to which you can put the technology is to "visit" distant parts of the universe, including the planets of other stars, and then of course the time-travel aspect of the device allows you to explore anywhere in history that interests you. Surely, after the novelty of Reality Porn had worn off, at least a sizable chunk of the population would be visiting the original Jurassic park or the rings of Saturn, or discovering what it was like to be bathed in the light of Andromedan suns? By the time our heroes are undertaking their journey back to the origin of life on earth, wouldn't millions of other people have already had the idea to take this same excursion? Likewise, there's a public project described earlier in the book to follow the life of Christ; but wouldn't all kinds of people, atheist and Xtian alike even if with differing motives, have thought of this almost immediately after the introduction of the technology? Why would there be the need for a project? (The chapter on this is called "Behold the Man", a perhaps unwise reminder of Mike Moorcock's significantly more ambitious time-travel treatment of the Passion.)<br /> 
</p><p>
  I raced through the first eighty or so pages of <i>The Light of Other Days</i>, finding in it a refreshing energy of ideas -- the kind of lure that used to make pulp sf so entrancing. Then, though, the other aspects of pulp sf began to get to me, in particular the pulpish plot and characterization (the tyrannical entrepreneur is like something out of a Batman comic), and thereafter I found myself labouring, rather. I still did like the gee-whiz ideas, and new ones kept appearing, so it wasn't an unrewarded slog; and I found the novel's resolution satisfying, however predictable it had by then become. Especially good was the introduction of the paranoia-inducing concept that, if anyone in our future ever invents the Wormcam or its equivalent, there's a reasonable chance that one of them is watching you <i>right now</i> -- or even <i>lots</i> of them.<br /> 
</p><p>All in all, then, the book's a curate's egg.</p></span>
  <a data-text-id="9773692668934592903" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_886081266" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/886081266" />
    <a title="James" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/29609954-james"><img alt="James" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1534698357p2/29609954.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/886081266?book_show_action=true">Mar 19, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James" href="/user/show/29609954-james">James</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">No one</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29609954-james?shelf=sf">sf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29609954-james?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer886081266" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4253293645068712623">This might be the worst novel I read this year, I certainly hope I don't read anything worse without being paid for it. First of this book is billed as a hard SF golden age, or as I like to say, bronze age, book and it lives up to its billing. First, it has the paper thin characters that characterized early SF. Even within these, there are horrible inconsistencies, why does Hiram, a paranoid control freak, hire Kate, a known enemy, to run a super-sensitive project? Why does Kate even go along wi</span>
  <span id="freeText4253293645068712623" style="display:none">This might be the worst novel I read this year, I certainly hope I don't read anything worse without being paid for it. First of this book is billed as a hard SF golden age, or as I like to say, bronze age, book and it lives up to its billing. First, it has the paper thin characters that characterized early SF. Even within these, there are horrible inconsistencies, why does Hiram, a paranoid control freak, hire Kate, a known enemy, to run a super-sensitive project? Why does Kate even go along with it? Because her character has no backbone? Why does Bobby fall in love with Kate? Can a thirty five year marriage based one night of great sex work? Because nothing else in the text furthers this romance. <br /><br />Next, plot, such as it is, jumps around in a random fashion with highly improbable activities, we have a widget than can track anybody, anywhere, any time, but we must of forgot how it works because Kate is kidnapped by Hiram for weeks. Hiram even brags about, they know Hiram has her! After being found by the magic widget she is rescued by the boyfriend, followed up by the FBI. It is standard police policy after all to let civilians lead hostage rescue missions. <br /><br />As for the hard science, while it may be theoretically possible for it to work, it's hard to believe that random teenagers are making better, much smaller, watch-sized WormCams in less than a year after the big giant room filling versions are created. Also the later WormCam attachments become too much like magical plot devices, invented when need to drive what little plot there is. Last but not least there are some Electric Chicken Plucker err.. WormCam science explanations that are just plain lame, John Campbell warned against these in the late 30's, shades of Hugo Gernsbeck!<br /><br />Finally there are the bizarre, historical digressions that occur in the story that don't support the plot and barely touch most of the characters in any form. In addition there is a long homage to Olaf Stapledon's "First and Last Man" which when I read it over forty years ago was fairly dated, which once again has no bearing on plot, the invention, or characters.<br /><br />Collaborations between authors are tough, I can only think of Niven and Pournelle along with Nordhoff and Hall that wrote better books together rather than individually. I'm not sure how much of this book's flaws can be chalked up to this. However I'm not planning on reading any of the other collaborations that they did together.<br /><br />I was expecting much more from this book, I've read better fanzine stuff.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4253293645068712623" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_787669874" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/787669874" />
    <a title="Dustin Sullivan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4007337-dustin-sullivan"><img alt="Dustin Sullivan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1413228577p2/4007337.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/787669874?book_show_action=true">Dec 11, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dustin Sullivan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dustin Sullivan" href="/user/show/4007337-dustin-sullivan">Dustin Sullivan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer787669874" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2817268447789262358">This was an interesting idea that was horribly executed. The characters are very flat. The story is not compelling. The story"telling" is the worst. Major plot points are basically skimmed over. <br/><br/>I also think the authors tried to address too many issues in one story. Not only are there WormCams, which allow anyone to view any point in spacetime, there's an asteroid on course to destroy the world in 500 years. Oh yeah, and people adapt the WormCam technology to link their minds and create some sor</span>
  <span id="freeText2817268447789262358" style="display:none">This was an interesting idea that was horribly executed. The characters are very flat. The story is not compelling. The story"telling" is the worst. Major plot points are basically skimmed over. <br /><br />I also think the authors tried to address too many issues in one story. Not only are there WormCams, which allow anyone to view any point in spacetime, there's an asteroid on course to destroy the world in 500 years. Oh yeah, and people adapt the WormCam technology to link their minds and create some sort of superconsciousness. Later, babies are born with wormholes already in their heads. Whatever.<br /><br />One big hurdle that I would think WormCam technology would need to overcome is that of a moving universe. To find a point in spacetime to view, you would have to account for the rotation of the Earth, the orbit of the Earth around the Sun, the trajectory of our galaxy, and the expansion of the universe. Not to mention getting a continuous stable view of the subject while all of those things are in motion. It bugs me that this was never addressed.<br /><br />There are huge plot holes that don't make any sense. For example, a character is falsely charged with a crime and they can't prove she didn't do it. Even though they have this all-seeing WormCam technology that can even look into the past. They talk about looking over her shoulder when she supposedly committed the crime, but somehow they can't get close enough to see what she was actually doing? It doesn't make any sense. Years later, she is cleared of the charge because they've developed technology to read hard drives through the WormCam. That's rather complex. What's so hard about reading a computer screen?<br /><br />About 5% of the end of the book is spent describing what some characters see as they rewind through time 4 billion years. Not only is it mostly inconsequential and not told in an interesting or believable manner, but it happens 40 years after they have the technology to do so. As if it never occurred to anyone to look that far back in time before.<br /><br />There's so much more that bugs me about this book, but I've already wasted enough time on it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2817268447789262358" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_165060384" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/165060384" />
    <a title="Greg Kennedy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1513354-greg-kennedy"><img alt="Greg Kennedy" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/165060384?book_show_action=true">May 02, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Greg Kennedy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Greg Kennedy" href="/user/show/1513354-greg-kennedy">Greg Kennedy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer165060384" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer563177849243629300">First the bad: It felt at times like a bizarre collision of cyberpunk and classic golden-age sci fi. The characters sucked big time. The pacing and focus sometimes drifted too much. I am maybe too squeamish about sex scenes, but this felt over the top. The backdrop and "near future" was nearly too far-fetched, before even reaching the heart of the story.<br/><br/>Yet this is a book that lives and dies by its central idea, and it's a damn good one - so good that after slogging through the first 80ish pages</span>
  <span id="freeText563177849243629300" style="display:none">First the bad: It felt at times like a bizarre collision of cyberpunk and classic golden-age sci fi. The characters sucked big time. The pacing and focus sometimes drifted too much. I am maybe too squeamish about sex scenes, but this felt over the top. The backdrop and "near future" was nearly too far-fetched, before even reaching the heart of the story.<br /><br />Yet this is a book that lives and dies by its central idea, and it's a damn good one - so good that after slogging through the first 80ish pages to reach the meat, it became nearly impossible to put down. This is one of those novels with an idea so absorbing, so potentially pervasive, that it is this which will leave you thinking for days later about the future and all its possibilities. It's been a while since I read this kind of sci-fi novel: one that says at its core simply "Here is a technology. Here are its societal effects". This is a welcome treat, and almost eerily more relevant than when it was published as the world has since developed the PATRIOT act, FaceBook, and the rise of mobile computing. At times unflinchingly frightening, while at other points reassuring and nearly inspiring. Highly recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="563177849243629300" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_247246299" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/247246299" />
    <a title="Lolly&#39;s Library" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2649865-lolly-s-library"><img alt="Lolly&#39;s Library" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1503734420p2/2649865.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/247246299?book_show_action=true">Dec 14, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lolly&#39;s Library" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lolly&#39;s Library" href="/user/show/2649865-lolly-s-library">Lolly&#39;s Library</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780312871994" href="/book/show/939179.The_Light_of_Other_Days">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2649865-lolly-s-library?shelf=did-not-finish">did-not-finish</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer247246299" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15027895984698310553">See, this is the problem with books written by "visionaries" who try to predict near-future events: When they get stuff wrong, it affects the entire reading experience. When you read a book published in 1950 and set in, say, 2000, it's easy to laugh at what the author thought the future would be (flying cars and regular trips to moon resorts, perhaps) and marvel at the things the author came close to getting right (perhaps a computer set-up very close to the internet or artificial bionic limbs).</span>
  <span id="freeText15027895984698310553" style="display:none">See, this is the problem with books written by "visionaries" who try to predict near-future events: When they get stuff wrong, it affects the entire reading experience. When you read a book published in 1950 and set in, say, 2000, it's easy to laugh at what the author thought the future would be (flying cars and regular trips to moon resorts, perhaps) and marvel at the things the author came close to getting right (perhaps a computer set-up very close to the internet or artificial bionic limbs). However, when a book is written in 2000, as <b>The Light of Other Days</b> was, and is set only thirty or so years in the future, it's a lot harder to laugh off the mistakes. TLoOD was written at time when the U.S. was riding high on its superiority: Everyone was rich and happy, the market was good, Dubya hadn't yet depleted all the budget surplus left to us by Clinton, and 9/11 wasn't even a plausible nightmare (except to those who worked in various alphabet-named agencies, who'd read the memos and seen the warning signs). Basically, America was still a great place to be. So of course, in that context, it makes sense that we would annex a faltering and weak United Kingdom and make it just another state in the union. Yet looking at that statement in our current climate of doubt, depression and fear, it's not only implausible, it's laughable. And that's the problem with the entire book: You get so caught up in the wrongness of the many predictions, it's hard to get past them to the actual plot. Then again, even from what little I read, there were other reasons making the book difficult to read. Mainly a lack of likable or compelling characters; everyone seemed to be venal, self-absorbed, irritating or just plain uninteresting. I've never read Clarke--he's one of those authors who I know I need to read, but I haven't yet gotten around to--so I really don't want to judge him based on this experience. I've never read Baxter either, in which case, it's hard to tell which author went wrong with this novel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15027895984698310553" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText12433966602158789330" style="display:none">This was a really good read!I got into reading Stephen Baxter's other two books, "Flood" &amp; "Ark" recently and loved them. When I saw that he wrote a book with Arthur C. Clarke, and the subject, I knew I would be in for a fun ride. They did not disappoint. What made this even more fun to read, was knowing that the book was written back in 2000. A lot of the ideas and technologies they wrote about are happening today, just with a different technology...the internet, web cams, streaming video etc...The various things that occur to society when all privacy is stripped away is fascinating to say the very least. It reminded me of another book I read years ago called "The Truth Machine" by James L. Halperin. In his book, a truth machine is invented, making it hard for anyone to lie, without being found out. The technological break through in "The light of other days" brings about a similar type of situation. Sit back, relax and enjoy the ride.</span>

<div id="review_1466514043" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1466514043" />
    <a title="Timothy Dymond" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/39472181-timothy-dymond"><img alt="Timothy Dymond" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1421975863p2/39472181.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1466514043?book_show_action=true">Jan 17, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Timothy Dymond" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Timothy Dymond" href="/user/show/39472181-timothy-dymond">Timothy Dymond</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780002247535" href="/book/show/1818044.The_Light_Of_Other_Days">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1466514043" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8147589092982394170">At the heart of ‘hard SF’ is a deep preoccupation with spirituality and meaning in the universe. ‘The Light Of Other Days’ demonstrates this by starting with what seems to be a story about the technological elimination of privacy, and finishes by contemplating a new version of humanity, and a new conception of human origins and ultimate purpose.<br/><br/>Such territory is nothing new for Arthur C Clarke or Stephen Baxter. They both exemplify the ‘science’ half of science fiction by writing characters that</span>
  <span id="freeText8147589092982394170" style="display:none">At the heart of ‘hard SF’ is a deep preoccupation with spirituality and meaning in the universe. ‘The Light Of Other Days’ demonstrates this by starting with what seems to be a story about the technological elimination of privacy, and finishes by contemplating a new version of humanity, and a new conception of human origins and ultimate purpose.<br /><br />Such territory is nothing new for Arthur C Clarke or Stephen Baxter. They both exemplify the ‘science’ half of science fiction by writing characters that function as walking ‘info-dump’ machines throughout the narrative. Of course there is a lot of information to dump, especially in the early parts of this book - which make it hard going for the first third. Clarke has always been a talented science educator, which means he has always been uncomfortable with ambiguity in his stories. Baxter is more comfortable with murky motivations. In this book it takes them a while to jointly smooth out their ‘tone’ for the characters, however some of that is down to the central conceit of the story - the use of ‘wormholes’ to view everything in both space and time - not really emerging until well into the book.<br /><br />A notable aspect of ‘Light Of Other Days’ is its relative pessimism about that traditional trope of hard SF - space travel. Throughout the book the Earth is under a looming threat of long term destruction by a huge interstellar object called ‘Wormwood’. In earlier hard SF stories this would be an invitation for humanity to get to work on an ambitious deflection, or interplanetary evacuation plan (particularly as they have five hundred years before the Wormwood collision). However in this story humanity reacts by having an existential crisis. The ‘wormhole’ devices that allow people see anything, about anyone, at anytime, leads people to become chronically self-absorbed. Indeed the theme of the story is people contemplating their own mortality - and trying to transcend it. The conclusion of the book offers a solution of sorts (not available to everyone) by humanity adopting a common, ‘joined’, consciousness in space, and exploring its origins in ‘deep’ time. Indeed the best part of the book is a journey via wormhole to the deep time origins of life itself, by travelling along the DNA of a single family. That section alone makes the book worth reading - but takes a while to get there.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8147589092982394170" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_461951698" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/461951698" />
    <a title="Peter" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14596076-peter"><img alt="Peter" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1352939150p2/14596076.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/461951698?book_show_action=true">Nov 23, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Peter" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Peter" href="/user/show/14596076-peter">Peter</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer461951698" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10186766514894558612">A revolution in wormhole technology for data transfer leads to another revolution... with a few modifications, a wormhole, invisible to the naked eye, can be opened anywhere in the world, and film what's going on at the other side. As privacy starts to erode, it soon becomes obvious that the wormholes can also look back to any point in the past, and the world must cope with the new technology and all the dangers and benefits it brings... like it has to do with any technology. <br/><br/>Some science fictio</span>
  <span id="freeText10186766514894558612" style="display:none">A revolution in wormhole technology for data transfer leads to another revolution... with a few modifications, a wormhole, invisible to the naked eye, can be opened anywhere in the world, and film what's going on at the other side. As privacy starts to erode, it soon becomes obvious that the wormholes can also look back to any point in the past, and the world must cope with the new technology and all the dangers and benefits it brings... like it has to do with any technology. <br /><br />Some science fiction stories are adventure tales with a science fiction setting, some are character stories using a fictional world and technology to tell very human stories, others use the technological advancement as a metaphor. And then there's the type where the authors imagine one development and attempt to ruthlessly project the impact it would have. This is a great example of one of those stories... the characters aren't bad, but they're also largely irrelevant, because really, the book is about how the technology changes the world. <br /><br />And while it makes a compelling case for many of the changes, it also serves as one of the other types, perhaps even by accident, because it's not JUST a fanciful 'what if this technology was real' but it also comments on our own world and feels remarkably prescient in some areas, particularly how ubiquitous smart phones and such shape society... many of the points about privacy can (with some modification and moderation) apply just as well to a world where everyone potentially carries a camera. <br /><br />There are some occasional big fumbles in believability... like, for example, somehow even after everyone has the ability to spy on everyone else, there are secrets maintained an unbelievably long time with nobody even seeming to go to extraordinary measures to conceal them... like for example the revelation that the wormholes can look back in time, seems to be known to a large number of people but the public doesn't find out until the author wants them to. Likewise, there's an instance with a person being accused of a crime and... they try to suggest that the now-present ability to look into the past somehow didn't resolve the issue, but it was never convincing to me, it seemed like any decent lawyer would examine certain key people's lives in great detail. Likewise, there are a few annoying dodges... like, I can totally understand why, in a section where great historical mysteries are answered using the technology, that the authors might not want to come down on certain religious issues, but it felt cheap to come up with an explanation that allowed that one event to somehow become impossible to view. <br /><br />And aside from that, there are a few other off notes... moments illustrating changes in sexual mores due to loss of privacy that I'm not entirely sure how I feel about for a variety of reasons, and there are parts where it just plain drags, like a backwards look at life on Earth that just goes on an on... I get a sense of wonder from the concept, but the actually telling of it was remarkably boring. <br /><br />Largely, though, and those bumps aside, the book just works. I'd read it many years ago (around when it first came out), and reading again I'm perhaps more impressed. I still find some of the character stuff a little on the weak side, and that might be a huge drawback to someone who isn't into the particular type of SF story this is meant to be, but it gave me what I wanted out of the premise. I'd probably rate it about 3.5 stars, were that option available, but it isn't, and I have to make a choice. And because it's a book that, in my heart, I'd rate as 'liked' rather than 'loved', and I doubt I'm going to read again anytime soon, I'm going to put it at 3 stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10186766514894558612" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_114722524" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/114722524" />
    <a title="Raj" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3320405-raj"><img alt="Raj" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1289899931p2/3320405.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/114722524?book_show_action=true">Aug 01, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Raj" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Raj" href="/user/show/3320405-raj">Raj</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780002247535" href="/book/show/1818044.The_Light_Of_Other_Days">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3320405-raj?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer114722524" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17698870214950351032">When I was a couple of chapters into this book, I felt that I was going to struggle with it, since I was finding the characters unmemorable (and, when I did remember them, irritating), the plot thin and none of the really big ideas that Clarke is famous for. I was wondering if this was just another senile-period damp squib. However, I'd heard good things about it, so I stuck with it and was eventually rewarded.<br/><br/>A driven media entrepreneur, Hiram Patterson, creates a way to use artificial wormhole</span>
  <span id="freeText17698870214950351032" style="display:none">When I was a couple of chapters into this book, I felt that I was going to struggle with it, since I was finding the characters unmemorable (and, when I did remember them, irritating), the plot thin and none of the really big ideas that Clarke is famous for. I was wondering if this was just another senile-period damp squib. However, I'd heard good things about it, so I stuck with it and was eventually rewarded.<br /><br />A driven media entrepreneur, Hiram Patterson, creates a way to use artificial wormholes to view any point on earth, and he uses this to scoop his media rivals. The book starts getting interesting once a) the 'wormcams' are able to look backward in time (due to the nature of spacetime equivalence at the quantum level, Patterson's genius son David realises that as well as moving in space, the wormholes can move in time) and b) the technology becomes democratised and available to the mass populous.<br /><br />It's at this point that the book starts tackling issues like the complete lack of privacy that becomes just about inevitable, and how now that everyone can become a peeping tom, society starts changing. We see extremes from a group called Refugees who use extreme technology to try and hide from the wormcam observers to the ones who go to the other extreme, eschewing any form of privacy, up to and including clothing (there's one scene that depicts a pair of teenagers having sex on a park bench in public, uncaring of the watchers).<br /><br />The book suggests that people eventually beyond this and start using the technology in large "wiki" projects to eradicate corruption and crime, and stripping the mythology of the past to see what historical figures were really like, rather than the myths that have built up around them. However, call me cynical, but I'm not sure that if we had access to such technology we'd ever get beyond the peeping tom phase, extrapolating from similar high hopes for the Internet.<br /><br />I continued to not find the human characters hugely interesting throughout the book, but couldn't ignore them for the ideas entirely. I suspect this may be Baxter's work, since Clarke's characters are often just narrative vehicles and entirely ignorable, but trying to force them to have their own story dragged the book down for me. The end, seeing Hiram's two sons, David and Bobby, 'travelling' far into earth's past as they followed their ancestors into geological time was a breathtaking journey showing me that Clarke still had what it takes to evoke my sense of wonder as effortlessly as he did forty years earlier.<br /><br />So, hard work in places, but definitely worth reading.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17698870214950351032" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_267924203" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/267924203" />
    <a title="Prashanth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7350584-prashanth"><img alt="Prashanth" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/267924203?book_show_action=true">Jan 26, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Prashanth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Prashanth" href="/user/show/7350584-prashanth">Prashanth</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780006483748" href="/book/show/64015.The_Light_Of_Other_Days">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer267924203&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer267924203" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3044138588664476521">I have a fond rememberance of Rama series by the author duo, which made me naturally gravitate towards this book when I saw it in JustBooks. One thing different from other SF is - this is NOT humans in space, and it has little role to play other than an asteroid due to collide with earth within 500 years. <br/><br/>There is a new invention in town - humans can watch any point in present or past through a worm-hole. You an imagine what happens - everyone watching everyone else, voyeurism at its peak, sexua</span>
  <span id="freeText3044138588664476521" style="display:none">I have a fond rememberance of Rama series by the author duo, which made me naturally gravitate towards this book when I saw it in JustBooks. One thing different from other SF is - this is NOT humans in space, and it has little role to play other than an asteroid due to collide with earth within 500 years. <br /><br />There is a new invention in town - humans can watch any point in present or past through a worm-hole. You an imagine what happens - everyone watching everyone else, voyeurism at its peak, sexual acts in public, disenchanted beings knowing their own past, and that of others around them. So, all sort of bad things happen, everyone is busy doing something or the other through this technology. The idea is interesting, but except for a few in-your-face human behaviour traits when in a world without secrets - this does not tell anything new. Afterall, what do you do when you can watch anything that is happening/happened on this planet? Explore how others look in the comfort of their own homes, in bathrooms, bedrooms and so on? I am surprised we go forward from that point at all. Left to me, I would see a disaster here - with everyone so occupied by what is happening around us, and what had happened to us. All this, but for a new set of advanced humans who simply latch this technology to their brain, so that they can communicate "seamlessly" with others in their own group. Humanity finally complete? - well, we won't know. <br /><br />The best part was the ending - the statement that draws attn to the fact that we started from microbes, which had been buried deep by methane-breathing beings on this very earth just before a meteor collision. <br />Coming back to the asteroid - we are talking about future for God's sake - dont we at least try a "armageddon" (the movie)? The asteroid has little to do with the overall story line (or, doomsday = more interest in the present/past?) . I simply came to believe this added more pages, and keeps user guessing.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3044138588664476521" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_109894804" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/109894804" />
    <a title="Bryan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3959858-bryan"><img alt="Bryan" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/109894804?book_show_action=true">Jul 03, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bryan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bryan" href="/user/show/3959858-bryan">Bryan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer109894804" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1633126272076071447">Pedaphilic pornographic garbage: I had to stop reading because I could not tolerate this novel any longer! I read 280 pages, and just couldn't finish it. Explicit sexual scenes seem to be the norm with Baxter these days. In this pornographic novel, Baxter describes two naked teenage children having sex in public, with adults watching them while pleasuring themselves. He goes into graphic detail. It's disgusting and shameful. Oh...it's all part of the story, and their behavior is just a result of</span>
  <span id="freeText1633126272076071447" style="display:none">Pedaphilic pornographic garbage: I had to stop reading because I could not tolerate this novel any longer! I read 280 pages, and just couldn't finish it. Explicit sexual scenes seem to be the norm with Baxter these days. In this pornographic novel, Baxter describes two naked teenage children having sex in public, with adults watching them while pleasuring themselves. He goes into graphic detail. It's disgusting and shameful. Oh...it's all part of the story, and their behavior is just a result of the key plot element. <br />That's an excuse to explore deviant behavior. <br /> <br />I am shocked that Clarke associated with this rubbish, because it reads nothing like a Clarke novel. For whatever reason, Baxter's publisher managed to get Clarke's name on the book. In my estimation, Clarke wrote or co-wrote the first chapter and that's all, because that was the only good part of the book! (BAIT AND SWITCH!). <br /> <br />I have completely turned 180 degrees from being a fan of Baxtor to absolutely loathing his garbage now. He had a good run early on, but has totally lost it. I loved Ring, The Time Ships, Flux, all the way up to Vacuum Diagrams. This novel, like all of his recent work, suffers from pathetically weak character development (I am so apathetic toward his characters, I can't recall their names). His use of questionable and outdated physics theories, and his obsession with explicit sexual scenes makes this all but intolerable to a true sci-fi fan. I will never read anything from this former author again. <br /></span>

<div id="review_804130245" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/804130245" />
    <a title="Cindy Matthews" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5202568-cindy-matthews"><img alt="Cindy Matthews" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442465330p2/5202568.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/804130245?book_show_action=true">Dec 29, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cindy Matthews" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cindy Matthews" href="/user/show/5202568-cindy-matthews">Cindy Matthews</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer804130245" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6906841595810248004">This is a book that I really wanted to like--tried to like throughout the long slog of reading it. I've always been a big fan of Clarke since his <i>Rendezvous with Rama</i> and <i>2001</i> days, but alas I can't say I enjoyed reading <i>The Light of Other Days</i> quite as much. I realize Clarke wasn't the best with developing three-dimensional characters as he is with explaining esoteric science theories, but is it too much to expect at least one character in a novel of this scope be someone you can cheer on? The </span>
  <span id="freeText6906841595810248004" style="display:none">This is a book that I really wanted to like--tried to like throughout the long slog of reading it. I've always been a big fan of Clarke since his <i>Rendezvous with Rama</i> and <i>2001</i> days, but alas I can't say I enjoyed reading <i>The Light of Other Days</i> quite as much. I realize Clarke wasn't the best with developing three-dimensional characters as he is with explaining esoteric science theories, but is it too much to expect at least one character in a novel of this scope be someone you can cheer on? The premise of the WormCam and exploring Earth's past and the social upheaval it causes is intriguing I'll admit. But when the reader is bombarded page after page of the authors' take on historical personalities and events with little attention given to the fact that on page one they've set a planet-killing asteroid directly into Earth's path... Well, your suspension of disbelief gets stretched a bit. You become irritated that you've been tricked into reading what could be equated as anti-religion propaganda and not a science fiction yarn. I expected something a bit better from these authors, frankly.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6906841595810248004" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer13190524190091910726">This is a well written, readable, and highly interesting science fiction read that speculates how humanity deals with the complete and total lose of privacy. Sadly, this book only gets 4 stars because its plot was not as good as Trigger. Overall, this book gave me some new ideas and concepts to explore. Some of the speculation by the authors became a bit painful at points because of the extended speculation (then again, science fiction is about speculation.)   <span id="freeText13190524190091910726" style="display:none">This is a well written, readable, and highly interesting science fiction read that speculates how humanity deals with the complete and total lose of privacy. Sadly, this book only gets 4 stars because its plot was not as good as Trigger. Overall, this book gave me some new ideas and concepts to explore. Some of the speculation by the authors became a bit painful at points because of the extended speculation (then again, science fiction is about speculation.) Overall, a shocking, thought provoking, and fun read that explored a large amount of varied issues in a easy to understand way. The light of other days is a good science fiction read because it uses the full power of fiction combinded with the factual basis of science to make it a incredible read. Recommended. </span>
